	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Wall Street</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Street in Manhattan, New York</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the street in Lower Manhattan. For the neighborhood commonly referred to as "Wall Street", see <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">Financial District, Manhattan</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Wall Street (disambiguation)">Wall Street (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Wall Street</b> is an eight-block-long street running roughly northwest to southeast from <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">Broadway</a> to <a href="/wiki/South_Street_(Manhattan)" title="South Street (Manhattan)">South Street</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">East River</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">Financial District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Over time, the term has become a <a href="/wiki/Metonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Metonym">metonym</a> for the financial markets of the United States as a whole, the <a href="/wiki/Financial_services_in_the_United_States" title="Financial services in the United States">American financial services industry</a> (even if financial firms are not physically located there), or New York–based financial interests.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anchored by Wall Street, New York City has been called both the most economically powerful city and the leading <a href="/wiki/Financial_center" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial center">financial center</a> of the world,<sup id="cite_ref-NYCDominantFinancialCenter_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYCDominantFinancialCenter-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EconomicallyPowerful2015_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconomicallyPowerful2015-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WorldEconomicAndFinancialSuperCenter_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEconomicAndFinancialSuperCenter-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and the city is home to the world's two <a href="/wiki/List_of_stock_exchanges" title="List of stock exchanges">largest stock exchanges</a> by total <a href="/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">market capitalization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYSEhighestcap_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYSEhighestcap-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYSElargest_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYSElargest-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Several other major exchanges have or had headquarters in the Wall Street area, including the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Board_of_Trade" title="New York Board of Trade">New York Board of Trade</a>, and the former <a href="/wiki/American_Stock_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="American Stock Exchange">American Stock Exchange</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#21st_century"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">21st century</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg/220px-CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg/330px-CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg/440px-CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CastelloPlanOriginal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original city map called the <a href="/wiki/Castello_Plan" title="Castello Plan">Castello Plan</a> from 1660, showing the wall on the right side</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG/220px-Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG/330px-Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG/440px-Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1690" data-file-height="987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_IRT_008c.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of the wall of New Amsterdam on a tile in the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">Wall Street</a> subway station, serving the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/4_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="4 (New York City Subway service)">4</a></span>&#32;and&#32;&#8203;<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/5_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="5 (New York City Subway service)">5</a></span>&#32;trains</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg/220px-The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg/330px-The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg/440px-The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2325" data-file-height="1824" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Vigilant_Stuyvesant%27s_Wall_Street_Gate_MET_ap61.79.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Vigilant Stuyvesant's Wall Street Gate</i>, 19th-century painting by <a href="/wiki/John_Quidor" title="John Quidor">John Quidor</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>There are varying accounts about how the Dutch-named "de Waalstraat"<sup id="cite_ref-let.rug.nl_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-let.rug.nl-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> got its name. A generally accepted version is that the name of the street was derived from a wall (actually a wooden palisade) on the northern boundary of the <a href="/wiki/New_Amsterdam" title="New Amsterdam">New Amsterdam</a> settlement, built to protect against Native Americans, pirates, and the British. A conflicting explanation is that Wall Street was named after <i><a href="/wiki/Walloons" title="Walloons">Walloons</a></i>—the Dutch name for a <i>Walloon</i> is <i>Waal</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-walloonswallets_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walloonswallets-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Among the first settlers that embarked on the ship "Nieu Nederlandt" in 1624 were 30 Walloon families. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Minuit" title="Peter Minuit">Peter Minuit</a>, the person who bought Manhattan for the Dutch, was a Walloon. While the Dutch word "wal" can be translated as "rampart", it only appeared as "de Walstraat" on English maps of New Amsterdam. However, even some English maps show the name as Waal Straat, and not as Wal Straat.<sup id="cite_ref-let.rug.nl_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-let.rug.nl-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>According to one version of the story:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The red people from Manhattan Island crossed to the mainland, where a treaty was made with the Dutch, and the place was therefore called the Pipe of Peace, in their language, Hoboken. But soon after that, the Dutch governor, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Kieft" title="Willem Kieft">Kieft</a>, sent his men out there one night and massacred the entire population. Few of them escaped, but they spread the story of what had been done, and this did much to antagonize all the remaining tribes against all the white settlers. Shortly after, Nieuw Amsterdam erected a double palisade for defense against its now enraged red neighbors, and this remained for some time the northern limit of the Dutch city. The space between the former walls is now called Wall Street, and its spirit is still that of a bulwark against the people.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In the 1640s basic picket and plank fences denoted plots and residences in the colony.<sup id="cite_ref-Sullivan_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Later, on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_West_India_Company" title="Dutch West India Company">Dutch West India Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Stuyvesant" title="Peter Stuyvesant">Peter Stuyvesant</a>, using both African slaves<sup id="cite_ref-White_New_Yorkers_in_Slave_Times_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White_New_Yorkers_in_Slave_Times-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/New_Netherlander" title="New Netherlander">white colonists</a>, collaborated with the city government in the construction of a more substantial <a href="/wiki/Fortifications_of_New_Netherland" title="Fortifications of New Netherland">fortification</a>, a strengthened 12-foot (4&#160;m) wall.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In 1685, surveyors laid out Wall Street along the lines of the original stockade.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_nyse_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline_nyse-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The wall started at Pearl Street, which was the shoreline at that time, crossing the Indian path Broadway and ending at the other shoreline (today's Trinity Place), where it took a turn south and ran along the shore until it ended at the old fort. In these early days, local merchants and traders would gather at disparate spots to buy and sell shares and bonds, and over time divided themselves into two classes—auctioneers and dealers.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanSb_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanSb-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Wall Street was also the marketplace where owners could hire out their slaves by the day or week.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Defensive_wall" title="Defensive wall">rampart</a> was removed in 1699<sup id="cite_ref-walloonswallets_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walloonswallets-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and a new City Hall built at Wall and Nassau in 1700.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">Slavery</a> was introduced to Manhattan in 1626, but it was not until December 13, 1711, that the New York City Common Council made Wall Street the city's first official slave market for the sale and rental of enslaved Africans and Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The slave market operated from 1711 to 1762 at the corner of Wall and Pearl Streets. It was a wooden structure with a roof and open sides, although walls may have been added over the years and could hold approximately 50 men. The city directly benefited from the sale of slaves by implementing taxes on every person who was bought and sold there.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 18th century there was a <a href="/wiki/American_sycamore" class="mw-redirect" title="American sycamore">buttonwood</a> tree at the foot of Wall Street under which <a href="/wiki/Trader_(finance)" title="Trader (finance)">traders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speculator" class="mw-redirect" title="Speculator">speculators</a> would gather to trade securities.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The benefit was being in proximity to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
In 1792, traders formalized their association with the <a href="/wiki/Buttonwood_Agreement" title="Buttonwood Agreement">Buttonwood Agreement</a> which was the origin of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The idea of the agreement was to make the market more "structured" and "without the manipulative auctions", with a commission structure.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanSb_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanSb-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Persons signing the agreement agreed to charge each other a standard commission rate; persons not signing could still participate but would be charged a higher commission for dealing.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanSb_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanSb-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg/220px-New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg/330px-New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg/440px-New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="690" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_York_City_Hall_1789b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conjectural view of Wall Street, as it probably looked at the time of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>'s inauguration, 1789</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tontine_coffee_house.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Tontine_coffee_house.jpg/220px-Tontine_coffee_house.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Tontine_coffee_house.jpg/330px-Tontine_coffee_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Tontine_coffee_house.jpg/440px-Tontine_coffee_house.jpg 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="402" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tontine_coffee_house.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tontine Coffee House, Wall Street in 1797</div></div></div>
<p>In 1789 Wall Street was the scene of the United States' first presidential inauguration when <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> took the oath of office on the balcony of <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall" title="Federal Hall">Federal Hall</a> on April 30, 1789. This was also the location of the passing of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill Of Rights</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, who was the first Treasury secretary and "architect of the early United States financial system," is buried in the cemetery of Trinity Church, as is <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fulton" title="Robert Fulton">Robert Fulton</a> famed for his <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg/220px-1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg/330px-1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg/440px-1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1596" data-file-height="1811" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1847_Lower_Manhattan_map.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1847 map showing the street layout and ferry routes for lower Manhattan</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_street_1867.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Wall_street_1867.jpg/220px-Wall_street_1867.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Wall_street_1867.jpg/330px-Wall_street_1867.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Wall_street_1867.jpg/440px-Wall_street_1867.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1552" data-file-height="988" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_street_1867.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of Wall Street from corner of Broad Street, 1867. On the left is the sub-Treasury building, now the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall_National_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Hall National Memorial">Federal Hall National Memorial</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the first few decades, both residences and businesses occupied the area, but increasingly business predominated. "There are old stories of people's houses being surrounded by the clamor of business and trade and the owners complaining that they can't get anything done," according to a historian named Burrows.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The opening of the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a> in the early 19th century meant a huge boom in business for New York City, since it was the only major eastern seaport which had direct access by inland waterways to ports on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>. Wall Street became the "money capital of America".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Charles R. Geisst suggested that there has constantly been a "tug-of-war" between business interests on Wall Street and authorities in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, the capital of the United States by then.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanSb_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanSb-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Generally during the 19th century Wall Street developed its own "unique personality and institutions" with little outside interference.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanSb_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanSb-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1840s and 1850s most residents moved further uptown to <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a> because of the increased business use at the lower tip of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Civil War had the effect of causing the northern economy to boom, bringing greater prosperity to cities like New York which "came into its own as the nation's banking center" connecting "Old World capital and New World ambition", according to one account.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._P._Morgan" title="J. P. Morgan">J. P. Morgan</a> created giant trusts; <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a> moved to New York.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Between 1860 and 1920, the economy changed from "agricultural to industrial to financial" and New York maintained its leadership position despite these changes, according to historian Thomas Kessner.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> New York was second only to London as the world's financial capital.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1884 <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Dow" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles H. Dow">Charles H. Dow</a> began tracking stocks, initially beginning with 11 stocks, mostly railroads, and looked at average prices for these eleven.<sup id="cite_ref-sether_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sether-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> When the average "peaks and troughs" went up consistently, he deemed it a bull market condition; if averages dropped, it was a bear market. He added up prices, and divided by the number of stocks to get his <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones average</a>. Dow's numbers were a "convenient benchmark" for analyzing the market and became an accepted way to look at the entire stock market. In 1889 the original stock report, <i>Customers' Afternoon Letter</i>, became <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>. Named in reference to the actual street, it became an influential international daily business newspaper published in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> After October 7, 1896, it began publishing Dow's expanded list of stocks.<sup id="cite_ref-sether_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sether-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A century later, there were 30 stocks in the average.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg/220px-Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg/330px-Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg/440px-Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_Manhattan_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wall Street around 1914</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wallstreetbmb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Wallstreetbmb.jpg/220px-Wallstreetbmb.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Wallstreetbmb.jpg/330px-Wallstreetbmb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Wallstreetbmb.jpg/440px-Wallstreetbmb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wallstreetbmb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wall Street Bombing 1920. Federal Hall National Memorial is at the right.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg/220px-Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg/330px-Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="314" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Depression-stock-market-crash-1929.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wall Street in the stock market crash of 1929</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg/220px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg/330px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg/440px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="606" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Wall_Street_Manhattan_-_George_Bradford_Brainerd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Picture of Wall Street, from the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a> collection</div></div></div>
<p>Business writer <a href="/wiki/John_Brooks_(writer)" title="John Brooks (writer)">John Brooks</a> in his book <i>Once in Golconda</i> considered the start of the 20th century period to have been Wall Street's heyday.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The address of 23 Wall Street, the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_%26_Co." title="J.P. Morgan &amp; Co.">J. P. Morgan &amp; Company</a>, known as <i>The Corner</i>, was "the precise center, geographical as well as metaphorical, of financial America and even of the financial world".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wall Street has had changing relationships with government authorities. In 1913, for example, when authorities proposed a $4 stock <a href="/wiki/Transfer_tax" title="Transfer tax">transfer tax</a>, stock clerks protested.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> At other times, city and state officials have taken steps through tax incentives to encourage financial firms to continue to do business in the city.
</p><p>In the late 19th and early 20th centuries the <a href="/wiki/Corporate_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporate culture">corporate culture</a> of New York was a primary center for the construction of <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>, and was rivaled only by <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago</a> on the American continent. There were also residential sections, such as the Bowling Green section between Broadway and the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a>, and between <a href="/wiki/Vesey_Street" title="Vesey Street">Vesey Street</a> and the Battery. The <a href="/wiki/Bowling_Green_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowling Green (Manhattan)">Bowling Green</a> area was described as "Wall Street's <a href="/wiki/Back_yard" class="mw-redirect" title="Back yard">back yard</a>" with poor people, high <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rates, and the "worst housing conditions in the city".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN412_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN412-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the construction, looking at New York City from the east, one can see two distinct clumps of tall buildings—the financial district on the left, and the taller <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">midtown</a> district on the right. The <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> of Manhattan is well-suited for tall buildings, with a solid mass of <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a> underneath Manhattan providing a firm foundation for tall buildings. Skyscrapers are expensive to build, but when there is a "short supply of land" in a "desirable location", then building upwards makes sound financial sense.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO44_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO44-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> A post office was built at <a href="/wiki/60_Wall_Street" title="60 Wall Street">60 Wall Street</a> in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO27_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO27-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> years, occasionally there were fund-raising efforts for projects such as the National Guard.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO14_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO14-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 16, 1920, close to the corner of Wall and Broad Street, the busiest corner of the financial district and across the offices of the <a href="/wiki/23_Wall_Street" title="23 Wall Street">Morgan Bank</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_bombing" title="Wall Street bombing">powerful bomb exploded</a>. It killed 38 and seriously injured 143 people.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The perpetrators were never identified or apprehended. The explosion did, however, help fuel the <a href="/wiki/Red_Scare" title="Red Scare">Red Scare</a> that was underway at the time. A report from <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The tomb-like silence that settles over Wall Street and lower Broadway with the coming of night and the suspension of business was entirely changed last night as hundreds of men worked under the glare of searchlights to repair the damage to skyscrapers that were lighted up from top to bottom. ... The Assay Office, nearest the point of explosion, naturally suffered the most. The front was pierced in fifty places where the cast iron slugs, which were of the material used for window weights, were thrown against it. Each slug penetrated the stone an inch or two and chipped off pieces ranging from three inches to a foot in diameter. The ornamental iron grill work protecting each window was broken or shattered. ... the Assay Office was a wreck. ... It was as though some gigantic force had overturned the building and then placed it upright again, leaving the framework uninjured but scrambling everything inside.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>1920<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN512_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN512-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The area was subjected to numerous threats; one bomb threat in 1921 led to detectives sealing off the area to "prevent a repetition of the Wall Street bomb explosion".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO16_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO16-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg/220px-Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg/330px-Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg/440px-Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="718" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crowd_outside_nyse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A crowd at Wall and Broad streets after the 1929 crash. The <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> is on the right. The majority of people are congregating in Wall Street on the left between the "House of Morgan" (<a href="/wiki/23_Wall_Street" title="23 Wall Street">23 Wall Street</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall_National_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Hall National Memorial">Federal Hall National Memorial</a> (26 Wall Street).</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>September 1929 was the peak of the stock market.<sup id="cite_ref-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> October 3, 1929 was when the market started to slip, and it continued throughout the week of the 14th.<sup id="cite_ref-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
In October 1929, renowned <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a> <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a> <a href="/wiki/Irving_Fisher" title="Irving Fisher">Irving Fisher</a> reassured worried investors that their "money was safe" on Wall Street.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO15a_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO15a-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> A few days later, on October 24,<sup id="cite_ref-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_world_in_depression_1929–1939-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> stock values plummeted. The <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">stock market crash of 1929</a> ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> in which a quarter of working people were unemployed, with soup kitchens, mass foreclosures of farms, and falling prices.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO15a_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO15a-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> During this era, development of the financial district stagnated, and Wall Street "paid a heavy price" and "became something of a backwater in American life".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO15a_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO15a-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> years as well as the 1940s, there was much less focus on Wall Street and finance. The government clamped down on the practice of buying equities based only on credit, but these policies began to ease. From 1946 to 1947, stocks could not be purchased "<a href="/wiki/Margin_(finance)" title="Margin (finance)">on margin</a>", meaning that an investor had to pay 100% of a stock's cost without taking on any loans.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO41a_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO41a-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, this margin requirement was reduced four times before 1960, each time stimulating a mini-rally and boosting volume, and when the Federal Reserve reduced the margin requirements from 90% to 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO41a_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO41a-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> These changes made it somewhat easier for investors to buy stocks on credit.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO41a_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO41a-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The growing national economy and prosperity led to a recovery during the 1960s, with some down years during the early 1970s in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. Trading volumes climbed; in 1967, according to <i><a href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Magazine">Time Magazine</a></i>, volume hit 7.5 million shares a day which caused a "traffic jam" of paper with "batteries of clerks" working overtime to "clear transactions and update customer accounts".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO28_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO28-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1973, the financial community posted a collective loss of $245 million, which spurred temporary help from the government.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO32a_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO32a-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Reforms were instituted; the SEC eliminated fixed commissions, which forced "brokers to compete freely with one another for investors' business".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO32a_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO32a-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1975, the Securities &amp; Exchange Commission threw out the NYSE's "Rule 394" which had required that "most stock transactions take place on the Big Board's floor", in effect freeing up trading for electronic methods.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO31_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO31-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 1976, banks were allowed to buy and sell stocks, which provided more competition for stockbrokers.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO31_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO31-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Reforms had the effect of lowering prices overall, making it easier for more people to participate in the stock market.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO31_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO31-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Broker commissions for each stock sale lessened, but volume increased.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO32a_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO32a-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan years</a> were marked by a renewed push for <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a>, with national efforts to de-regulate industries such as <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a>. The economy resumed upward growth after a period in the early 1980s of languishing. A report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> described that the flushness of money and growth during these years had spawned a drug culture of sorts, with a rampant acceptance of cocaine use although the overall percent of actual users was most likely small. A reporter wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Wall Street drug dealer looked like many other successful young female executives. Stylishly dressed and wearing designer sunglasses, she sat in her 1983 Chevrolet Camaro in a no-parking zone across the street from the Marine Midland Bank branch on lower Broadway. The customer in the passenger seat looked like a successful young businessman. But as the dealer slipped him a heat-sealed plastic envelope of cocaine and he passed her cash, the transaction was being watched through the sunroof of her car by Federal drug agents in a nearby building. And the customer&#160;— an undercover agent himself -was learning the ways, the wiles and the conventions of Wall Street's drug subculture.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Peter Kerr in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO22_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO22-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1_Wall_Street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/1_Wall_Street.jpg/200px-1_Wall_Street.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/1_Wall_Street.jpg/300px-1_Wall_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/1_Wall_Street.jpg/400px-1_Wall_Street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1680" data-file-height="2240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1_Wall_Street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/1_Wall_Street" title="1 Wall Street">1 Wall Street</a>, at Wall Street and Broadway</div></div></div>
<p>In 1987 the stock market plunged<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and, in the relatively brief recession following, lower Manhattan lost 100,000 jobs according to one estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN211_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN211-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Since telecommunications costs were coming down, banks and <a href="/wiki/Brokerage_firm" class="mw-redirect" title="Brokerage firm">brokerage firms</a> could move away from Wall Street to more affordable locations.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN211_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN211-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The recession of 1990–91 was marked by office vacancy rates downtown which were "persistently high" and with some buildings "standing empty".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The day of the stock market drop, October 20, was marked by "stony-faced traders whose sense of humor had abandoned them and in the exhaustion of stock exchange employees struggling to maintain orderly trading".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Ironically, it was the same year that Oliver Stone's movie <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_(1987_film)" title="Wall Street (1987 film)">Wall Street</a></i> appeared. In 1995, city authorities offered the <i>Lower Manhattan Revitalization Plan</i> which offered incentives to convert commercial properties to residential use.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Construction of the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> began in 1966 but had trouble attracting tenants when completed. Nonetheless, some substantial firms purchased space there. Its impressive height helped make it a visual landmark for drivers and pedestrians. In some respects, the nexus of the financial district moved from the <i>street</i> of Wall Street to the Trade Center complex. Real estate growth during the latter part of the 1990s was significant, with deals and new projects happening in the financial district and elsewhere in Manhattan; one firm invested more than $24 billion in various projects, many in the Wall Street area.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO24_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO24-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, the NYSE and the city struck a $900 million deal which kept the NYSE from moving across the river to <a href="/wiki/Jersey_City,_New_Jersey" title="Jersey City, New Jersey">Jersey City</a>; the deal was described as the "largest in city history to prevent a corporation from leaving town".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO34_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO34-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A competitor to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">NYSE</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a>, moved its headquarters from Washington to New York.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO36a_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO36a-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG/250px-Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG/375px-Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG/500px-Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_Manhattan_Aerial.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wall Street as seen from the air in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>In 2001 the <i>Big Board</i>, as some termed the NYSE, was described as the world's "largest and most prestigious stock market".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO35a_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO35a-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> But when the World Trade Center was destroyed on <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001</a>, it left an architectural void as new developments since the 1970s had played off the complex aesthetically. The attacks "crippled" the communications network.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO35a_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO35a-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> One estimate was that 45% of Wall Street's "best office space" had been lost.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The physical destruction was immense:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Debris littered some streets of the financial district. National Guard members in camouflage uniforms manned checkpoints. Abandoned coffee carts, glazed with dust from the collapse of the World Trade Center, lay on their sides across sidewalks. Most subway stations were closed, most lights were still off, most telephones did not work, and only a handful of people walked in the narrow canyons of Wall Street yesterday morning.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Leslie Eaton and Kirk Johnson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, September 16, 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO18a_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO18a-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Still, the NYSE was determined to re-open on September 17, almost a week after the attack.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO18a_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO18a-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> During this time <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Group_Business_Center" title="Rockefeller Group Business Center">Rockefeller Group Business Center</a> opened additional offices at 48 Wall Street. The attack hastened a trend towards financial firms moving to midtown and contributed to the loss of business on Wall Street, due to temporary-to-permanent relocation to <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and further decentralization with establishments transferred to cities like <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denver,_Colorado" class="mw-redirect" title="Denver, Colorado">Denver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston</a>.
</p><p>After September 11, the financial services industry went through a downturn with a sizable drop in year-end bonuses of $6.5 billion, according to one estimate from a state comptroller's office.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Many brokers are paid mostly through commission, and get a token annual salary which is dwarfed by the year-end bonus.
</p><p>To guard against a vehicular bombing in the area, authorities built concrete barriers, and found ways over time to make them more aesthetically appealing by spending $5000 to $8000 apiece on <a href="/wiki/Bollard" title="Bollard">bollards</a>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To prevent a vehicle-delivered bomb from entering the area, Rogers Marvel designed a new kind of bollard, a faceted piece of sculpture whose broad, slanting surfaces offer people a place to sit in contrast to the typical bollard, which is supremely unsittable. The bollard, which is called the Nogo, looks a bit like one of Frank Gehry's unorthodox culture palaces, but it is hardly insensitive to its surroundings. Its bronze surfaces actually echo the grand doorways of Wall Street's temples of commerce. Pedestrians easily slip through groups of them as they make their way onto Wall Street from the area around historic Trinity Church. Cars, however, cannot pass.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Blair Kamin in the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i>, 2006<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN513_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN513-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Wall Street itself and the Financial District as a whole are crowded with highrises. Further, the loss of the World Trade Center has spurred development on a scale that had not been seen in decades. In 2006, Goldman Sachs began building a tower near the former Trade Center site.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO44_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO44-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Tax incentives provided by federal, state and local governments encouraged development. A new World Trade Center complex centered on <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Libeskind" title="Daniel Libeskind">Daniel Libeskind</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Memory_Foundations" title="Memory Foundations">Memory Foundations</a> and was under development after the 9/11 attacks. The centerpiece, which is now a 1,776-foot (541&#160;m) tall structure, opened in 2014 and named the <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">One World Trade Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> New residential buildings are sprouting up, and buildings that were previously office space are being converted to residential units, also benefiting from tax incentives. A new <a href="/wiki/Fulton_Center" title="Fulton Center">Fulton Center</a>, which was planned to improve access to the area, opened in 2014. In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Maharishi_Mahesh_Yogi" title="Maharishi Mahesh Yogi">Maharishi Global Financial Capital of New York</a> opened headquarters at 70 Broad Street near the NYSE, in an effort to seek investors.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO17_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO17-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reporter Andrew Clark described the years of 2006 to 2010 as "tumultuous" in which the heartland of America is "mired in gloom" with high unemployment around 9.6%, with average house prices falling from $230,000 in 2006 to $183,000, and foreboding increases in the national debt to $13.4 trillion, but that despite the setbacks, the American economy was once more "bouncing back".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> What had happened during these heady years? Clark wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But the picture is too nuanced simply to dump all the responsibility on financiers. Most Wall Street banks didn't actually go around the US hawking dodgy mortgages; they bought and packaged loans from on-the-ground firms such as Countrywide Financial and New Century Financial, both of which hit a financial wall in the crisis. Foolishly and recklessly, the banks didn't look at these loans adequately, relying on flawed credit-rating agencies such as Standard &amp; Poor's and Moody's, which blithely certified toxic mortgage-backed securities as solid ... A few of those on Wall Street, including maverick hedge fund manager John Paulson and the top brass at Goldman Sachs, spotted what was going on and ruthlessly gambled on a crash. They made a fortune but turned into the crisis's pantomime villains. Most, though, got burned&#160;– the banks are still gradually running down portfolios of non-core loans worth $800bn.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reporter Andrew Clark, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG/200px-Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG/300px-Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG/400px-Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_and_Trinity_Church_2014.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Looking west toward <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(Manhattan)" title="Trinity Church (Manhattan)">Trinity Church</a>; a crowd on Wall Street is pictured in the foreground</div></div></div>
<p>The first months of 2008 was a particularly troublesome period which caused <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bernanke" title="Ben Bernanke">Ben Bernanke</a> to "work holidays and weekends" and which did an "extraordinary series of moves".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO37_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO37-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> It bolstered U.S. banks and allowed Wall Street firms to borrow "directly from the Fed".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO37_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO37-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These efforts were highly controversial at the time, but from the perspective of 2010, it appeared the Federal exertions had been the right decisions. By 2010, Wall Street firms, in Clark's view, were "getting back to their old selves as engine rooms of wealth, prosperity and excess".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> A report by Michael Stoler in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Sun" title="The New York Sun">The New York Sun</a></i> described a "phoenix-like resurrection" of the area, with residential, commercial, retail and hotels booming in the "third largest business district in the country".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO19a_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO19a-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, the investment community was worried about proposed legal reforms, including the <i>Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</i> which dealt with matters such as credit card rates and lending requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO42_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO42-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The NYSE closed two of its trading floors in a move towards transforming itself into an electronic exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in September 2011, <a href="/wiki/Occupy_Wall_Street" title="Occupy Wall Street">demonstrators</a> disenchanted with the financial system protested in parks and plazas around Wall Street.<sup id="cite_ref-twsP44f545a_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsP44f545a-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wall Street <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> fees in 2012 totaled approximately $40 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> while senior bank officers managing <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk and compliance</a> functions earned as much as $324,000 annually in New York City in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 29, 2012, Wall Street was disrupted when New York and New Jersey were inundated by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">Hurricane Sandy</a>. Its 14-foot-high storm surge, a local record, caused massive street flooding in many parts of Lower Manhattan. Power to the area was knocked out by a transformer explosion at a <a href="/wiki/Con_Edison" class="mw-redirect" title="Con Edison">Con Edison</a> plant. With mass transit in New York City already suspended as a precaution even before the storm hit, the New York Stock Exchange and other financial exchanges were closed for two days, re-opening on Oct. 31.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> It was the first weather-related closing for the NYSE since <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Gloria" title="Hurricane Gloria">Hurricane Gloria</a> in September 1985 and the first two-day weather-related shutdown since the <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_of_1888" class="mw-redirect" title="Blizzard of 1888">Blizzard of 1888</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG/220px-Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG/330px-Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG/440px-Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Federal_Hall_and_George_Washington_statue_in_New_York_City.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall_National_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Hall National Memorial">Federal Hall National Memorial</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG/220px-USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG/330px-USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG/440px-USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USA-NYC-New_York_Stock_Exchange.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of New York Stock Exchange</div></div></div>
<p>Wall Street's architecture is generally rooted in the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a2_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Landmark buildings on Wall Street include <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall_National_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Hall National Memorial">Federal Hall National Memorial</a>, <a href="/wiki/14_Wall_Street" title="14 Wall Street">14 Wall Street</a> (Bankers Trust Company Building), <a href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">40 Wall Street</a> (The Trump Building), <a href="/wiki/55_Wall_Street" title="55 Wall Street">55 Wall Street</a> (the former world headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Citicorp" class="mw-redirect" title="Citicorp">Citicorp</a>), the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> at the corner of <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Broad Street (Manhattan)">Broad Street</a> and the US headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> at <a href="/wiki/60_Wall_Street" title="60 Wall Street">60 Wall Street</a>. The Deutsche Bank building (formerly the <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_%26_Co." title="J.P. Morgan &amp; Co.">J.P. Morgan</a> headquarters) makes Deutsche Bank the last remaining major investment bank to have its headquarters on Wall Street.
</p><p>The older skyscrapers often were built with elaborate facade, which have not been common in corporate architecture for decades; the nearby  World Trade Center, built in the 1970s, was very plain and utilitarian in comparison. Excavation from the World Trade Center was later used as landfill for <a href="/wiki/Battery_Park_City" title="Battery Park City">Battery Park City</a> residential developments.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/23_Wall_Street" title="23 Wall Street">23 Wall Street</a>, built in 1914, was known as the "<a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase_%26_Co." class="mw-redirect" title="JPMorgan Chase &amp; Co.">House of Morgan</a>" and served for decades as the bank's headquarters and, by some accounts, was viewed as an important address in American finance. Cosmetic damage from the 1920 <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_bombing" title="Wall Street bombing">Wall Street bombing</a> is still visible on the Wall Street side of this building.
</p><p>Another key anchor for the area is the New York Stock Exchange. City authorities realize its importance, and believed that it has "outgrown its neoclassical temple at the corner of Wall and Broad streets", and in 1998 offered substantial tax incentives to try to keep it in the Financial District.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Plans to rebuild it were delayed by the September 11, 2001 attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The exchange still occupies the same site. The exchange is the locus for a large amount of technology and data. For example, to accommodate the three thousand persons who work directly on the Exchange floor requires 3,500 kilowatts of electricity, along with 8,000 phone circuits on the trading floor alone, and 200 miles of fiber-optic cable below ground.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO18a_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO18a-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance">Importance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Importance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_economic_engine">As an economic engine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: As an economic engine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_New_York_economy">In the New York economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In the New York economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Finance professor Charles R. Geisst wrote that the exchange has become "inextricably intertwined into New York's economy".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO35a_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO35a-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Wall Street pay, in terms of salaries and bonuses and taxes, is an important part of the economy of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tri-State_area_(NY-NJ-CT)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tri-State area (NY-NJ-CT)">tri-state metropolitan area</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States" title="Economy of the United States">United States</a>. In 2008, after a downturn in the stock market, the decline meant $18 billion less in taxable income, with less money available for "apartments, furniture, cars, clothing and services".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214a_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214a-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> A falloff in Wall Street's economy could have "wrenching effects on the local and regional economies".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214a_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214a-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates vary about the number and quality of financial jobs in the city. One estimate was that Wall Street firms employed close to 200,000 persons in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214a_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214a-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Another estimate was that in 2007, the financial services industry which had a $70 billion profit became 22 percent of the city's revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO23b_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO23b-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Another estimate (in 2006) was that the financial services industry makes up 9% of the city's work force and 31% of the tax base.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO46_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO46-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> An additional estimate (2007) from Steve Malanga of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Manhattan Institute">Manhattan Institute</a> was that the securities industry accounts for 4.7 percent of the jobs in New York City but 20.7 percent of its wages, and he estimated there were 175,000 securities-industries jobs in New York (both Wall Street area and midtown) paying an average of $350,000 annually.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Between 1995 and 2005, the sector grew at an annual rate of about 6.6% annually, a respectable rate, but that other financial centers were growing faster.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Another estimate (2008) was that Wall Street provided a fourth of all personal income earned in the city, and 10% of New York City's tax revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJan62O_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJan62O-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>  The city's securities industry, enumerating 163,400 jobs in August 2013, continues to form the largest segment of the city's financial sector and an important economic engine, accounting in 2012 for 5 percent of private sector jobs in New York City, 8.5 percent (US$3.8 billion) of the city's tax revenue, and 22 percent of the city's total wages, including an average salary of US$360,700.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The seven largest Wall Street firms in the 2000s were <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a>, <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Incorporated" class="mw-redirect" title="Citigroup Incorporated">Citigroup Incorporated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214a_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214a-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> During the recession of 2008–10, many of these firms, including Lehman, went out of business or were bought up at firesale prices by other financial firms. In 2008, Lehman filed for bankruptcy,<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> was bought by <a href="/wiki/JP_Morgan_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="JP Morgan Chase">JP Morgan Chase</a><sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> forced by the U.S. government,<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO37_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO37-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> was bought by <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a> in a similar shot-gun wedding. These failures marked a catastrophic downsizing of Wall Street as the financial industry goes through restructuring and change. Since New York's financial industry provides almost one-fourth of all income produced in the city, and accounts for 10% of the city's tax revenues and 20% of the state's, the downturn has had huge repercussions for government treasuries.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN214a_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN214a-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> New York's mayor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bloomberg" title="Michael Bloomberg">Michael Bloomberg</a> reportedly over a four-year period dangled over $100 million in tax incentives to persuade Goldman Sachs to build a 43-story headquarters in the financial district near the destroyed World Trade Center site.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO23b_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO23b-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, things looked somewhat gloomy, with one analysis by the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Consulting_Group" title="Boston Consulting Group">Boston Consulting Group</a> suggesting that 65,000 jobs had been permanently lost because of the downturn.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO23b_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO23b-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> But there were signs that Manhattan property prices were rebounding with price rises of 9% annually in 2010, and bonuses were being paid once more, with average bonuses over $124,000 in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Versus_Midtown_Manhattan">Versus Midtown Manhattan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Versus Midtown Manhattan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A requirement of the New York Stock Exchange was that brokerage firms had to have offices "clustered around Wall Street" so clerks could deliver physical paper copies of stock certificates each week.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> There were some indications that midtown had been becoming the locus of financial services dealings even by 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215xx_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215xx-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> But as technology progressed, in the middle and later decades of the 20th century, computers and telecommunications replaced paper notifications, meaning that the close proximity requirement could be bypassed in more situations.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Many financial firms found that they could move to midtown Manhattan four miles away<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN312a_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN312a-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> or elsewhere and still operate effectively. For example, the former investment firm of <a href="/wiki/Donaldson,_Lufkin_%26_Jenrette" title="Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette">Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette</a> was described as a <i>Wall Street firm</i> but had its headquarters on Park Avenue in midtown.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN212_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN212-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> A report described the migration from Wall Street:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The financial industry has been slowly migrating from its historic home in the warren of streets around Wall Street to the more spacious and glamorous office towers of Midtown Manhattan. Morgan Stanley, J.P. Morgan Chase, Citigroup, and Bear Stearns have all moved north.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><i>USA Today</i>, October 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Nevertheless, a key magnet for the Wall Street remains the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>. Some "old guard" firms such as <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> (bought by <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a> in 2009), have remained "fiercely loyal to the financial district" location, and new ones such as <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> have chosen office space in the district.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> So-called "face-to-face" trading between buyers and sellers remains a "cornerstone" of the NYSE, with a benefit of having all of a deal's players close at hand, including <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment bankers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">lawyers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Accountant" title="Accountant">accountants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN215a_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN215a-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, the Manhattan <a href="/wiki/Financial_District_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial District (Manhattan)">Financial District</a> is one of the largest business districts in the United States, and second in New York City only to <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown</a> in terms of dollar volume of business transacted.
</p>
