<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_New_Jersey_economy">In the New Jersey economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In the New Jersey economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_West" title="Wall Street West">Wall Street West</a></div>
<p>After Wall Street firms started to expand westward in the 1980s to <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> the direct economic impacts of Wall Street activities have gone beyond New York City. The employment in the financial services industry mostly in the "back office" roles has become an important part of New Jersey economy.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, the Wall Street employment wages were paid in the amount of almost $18.5 billion in the state. The industry contributed $39.4 billion or 8.4 percent to the New Jersey's <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> in the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-njfinance_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-njfinance-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most significant area with Wall Street employment is in <a href="/wiki/Jersey_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Jersey City">Jersey City</a>. In 2008, the "Wall Street West" employment contributed to one third of the <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a> jobs in Jersey City. Within the Financial Service cluster, there were three major sectors: more than 60 percent were in the <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities industry</a>; 20 percent were in <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">banking</a>; and 8 percent in <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, New Jersey has become the main technology infrastructure to support the Wall Street operations. A substantial amount of securities traded in the United States are executed in New Jersey as the <a href="/wiki/Data_center" title="Data center">data centers</a> of electronic trading in the U.S. equity market for all major stock exchanges are located in <a href="/wiki/North_Jersey" title="North Jersey">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Jersey" title="Central Jersey">Central Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> A significant amount of securities <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">clearing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Settlement_(finance)" title="Settlement (finance)">settlement</a> workforce is also in the state. This includes the majority of the workforce of Depository Trust Company,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> the primary U.S. securities <a href="/wiki/Depository_bank" title="Depository bank">depository</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Depository_Trust_%26_Clearing_Corporation" title="Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation">Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> the parent company of National Securities Clearing Corporation, the Fixed Income Clearing Corporation and Emerging Markets Clearing Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having a direct tie to Wall Street employment is problematic for New Jersey, however. The state lost 7.9 percent of its employment base from 2007 to 2010 in the financial services sector in the fallout of the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-njfinance_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-njfinance-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Competing_financial_centers">Competing financial centers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Competing financial centers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Financial_centre" title="Financial centre">Financial centre</a></div>
<p>Of the street's importance as a financial center, <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> analyst <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Gross" title="Daniel Gross">Daniel Gross</a> wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In today's burgeoning and increasingly integrated global financial markets&#160;— a vast, neural spaghetti of wires, Web sites and trading platforms&#160;— the N.Y.S.E. is clearly no longer the epicenter. Nor is New York. The largest mutual-fund complexes are in <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge,_Pennsylvania" title="Valley Forge, Pennsylvania">Valley Forge, Pa.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, while trading and money management are spreading globally. Since the end of the cold war, vast pools of capital have been forming overseas, in the Swiss bank accounts of Russian oligarchs, in the Shanghai vaults of Chinese manufacturing magnates and in the coffers of funds controlled by governments in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> that may amount to some $2.5 trillion.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Daniel Gross in 2007<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>An example is the alternative trading platform known as <a href="/wiki/BATS_Global_Markets" title="BATS Global Markets">BATS</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City">Kansas City</a>, which came "out of nowhere to gain a 9 percent share in the market for trading United States stocks".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The firm has computers in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, two salespersons in New York City, but the remaining 33 employees work in a center in Kansas.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO53a_26-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO53a-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_public_imagination">In the public imagination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In the public imagination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG/220px-Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG/330px-Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG/440px-Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1872" data-file-height="2496" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Church_NYC_004b.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(New_York_City)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity Church (New York City)">Trinity Church</a> from Wall Street.</div></div></div>
<p>Wall Street in a conceptual sense represents financial and economic power. To Americans, it can sometimes represent elitism and power politics, and its role has been a source of controversy throughout the nation's history, particularly beginning around the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a> period in the late 19th century. Wall Street became the symbol of a country and economic system that many Americans see as having developed through trade, capitalism, and innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wall Street has become synonymous with financial interests, often used negatively.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN112_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN112-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a> from 2007–10, Wall Street financing was blamed as one of the causes, although most commentators blame an interplay of factors. The U.S. government with the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> bailed out the banks and financial backers with billions of taxpayer dollars, but the bailout was often criticized as politically motivated,<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN112_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN112-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and was criticized by journalists as well as the public. Analyst <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kuttner" title="Robert Kuttner">Robert Kuttner</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Huffington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Huffington Post">Huffington Post</a></i> criticized the bailout as helping large Wall Street firms such as Citigroup while neglecting to help smaller community development banks such as Chicago's <a href="/wiki/ShoreBank" title="ShoreBank">ShoreBank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN112_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN112-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> One writer in the <i>Huffington Post</i> looked at FBI statistics on robbery, fraud, and crime and concluded that Wall Street was the "most dangerous neighborhood in the United States" if one factored in the $50 billion <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a> perpetrated by <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Madoff" title="Bernie Madoff">Bernie Madoff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When large firms such as <a href="/wiki/Enron" title="Enron">Enron</a>, <a href="/wiki/WorldCom" class="mw-redirect" title="WorldCom">WorldCom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_Crossing" title="Global Crossing">Global Crossing</a> were found guilty of fraud, Wall Street was often blamed,<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO15a_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO15a-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> even though these firms had headquarters around the nation and not in Wall Street. Many complained that the resulting <a href="/wiki/Sarbanes-Oxley" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarbanes-Oxley">Sarbanes-Oxley</a> legislation dampened the business climate with regulations that were "overly burdensome".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO54_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO54-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Interest groups seeking favor with <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington lawmakers</a>, such as car dealers, have often sought to portray their interests as allied with <i>Main Street</i> rather than <i>Wall Street</i>, although analyst Peter Overby on <i><a href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="National Public Radio">National Public Radio</a></i> suggested that car dealers have written over $250 billion in consumer loans and have real ties with <i>Wall Street</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN411_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN411-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Treasury">United States Treasury</a> bailed out large financial firms, to ostensibly halt a downward spiral in the nation's economy, there was tremendous negative political fallout, particularly when reports came out that monies supposed to be used to ease credit restrictions were being used to pay bonuses to highly paid employees.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Analyst William D. Cohan argued that it was "obscene" how Wall Street reaped "massive profits and bonuses in 2009" after being saved by "trillions of dollars of American taxpayers' treasure" despite Wall Street's "greed and irresponsible risk-taking".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO43_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO43-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> reporter Suzanne McGee called for Wall Street to make a sort of public apology to the nation, and expressed dismay that people such as <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> chief executive <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a> hadn't expressed contrition despite being sued by the <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a> in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO26_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO26-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> McGee wrote that "Bankers aren't the sole culprits, but their too-glib denials of responsibility and the occasional vague and waffling expression of regret don't go far enough to deflect anger."<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO26_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO26-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:60_Wall_Street_building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/60_Wall_Street_building.jpg/220px-60_Wall_Street_building.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/60_Wall_Street_building.jpg/330px-60_Wall_Street_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/60_Wall_Street_building.jpg/440px-60_Wall_Street_building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:60_Wall_Street_building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> on Wall Street in 2010.</div></div></div>
<p>But chief banking analyst at <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>, Richard Ramsden, is "unapologetic" and sees "banks as the dynamos that power the rest of the economy".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Ramsden believes "risk-taking is vital" and said in 2010:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You can construct a banking system in which no bank will ever fail, in which there's no leverage. But there would be a cost. There would be virtually no economic growth because there would be no credit creation.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Richard Ramsden of Goldman Sachs, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Others in the financial industry believe they've been unfairly castigated by the public and by politicians. For example, Anthony Scaramucci reportedly told President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in 2010 that he felt like a <a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1ata" title="Piñata">piñata</a>, "whacked with a stick" by "hostile politicians".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanO12a_57-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanO12a-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The financial misdeeds of various figures throughout American history sometimes casts a dark shadow on financial investing as a whole, and include names such as <a href="/wiki/William_Duer_(Continental_Congressman)" title="William Duer (Continental Congressman)">William Duer</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Fisk_(financier)" title="James Fisk (financier)">Jim Fisk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a> (the latter two believed to have been involved with an effort to collapse the U.S. gold market in 1869) as well as modern figures such as <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Madoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard Madoff">Bernard Madoff</a> who "bilked billions from investors".<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN413_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN413-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, images of Wall Street and its figures have loomed large. The 1987 <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Stone" title="Oliver Stone">Oliver Stone</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_(1987_film)" title="Wall Street (1987 film)">Wall Street</a></i> created the iconic figure of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Gekko" title="Gordon Gekko">Gordon Gekko</a> who used the phrase "greed is good", which caught on in the cultural parlance.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN514_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN514-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Stone commented in 2009 how the movie had had an unexpected cultural influence, not causing them to turn away from corporate greed, but causing many young people to choose Wall Street careers because of that movie.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN514_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN514-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> A reporter repeated other lines from the film: "I'm talking about liquid. Rich enough to have your own jet. Rich enough not to waste time. Fifty, a hundred million dollars, Buddy. A player."<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN514_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN514-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wall Street firms have however also contributed to projects such as <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a> as well as done food programs in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> and trauma centers in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and rescue boats during floods in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-twsJanN111_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsJanN111-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Versus_Main_Street">Versus Main Street</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Versus Main Street">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG/220px-Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG/330px-Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG/440px-Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1779" data-file-height="1997" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wall_Street_%26_Broadway.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Not just a <a href="/wiki/Metonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Metonym">metonym</a>, Wall Street also has its own street sign.</div></div></div>
<p>As a figure of speech contrasted to "<a href="/wiki/Main_Street" title="Main Street">Main Street</a>", the term "Wall Street" can refer to big business interests against those of small business and the working or middle classes. It is sometimes used more specifically to refer to research analysts, shareholders, and financial institutions such as investment banks. Whereas "Main Street" conjures up images of locally owned businesses and banks, the phrase "Wall Street" is commonly used interchangeably with the phrase "Corporate America". It is also sometimes used in contrast to distinguish between the interests, culture, and lifestyles of investment banks and those of <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> industrial or service corporations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_culture">As a culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: As a culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairbanks_and_Chaplin,_Wall_Street_Rally,_New_York_Times,_1918.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG/220px-Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG/330px-Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG/440px-Fairbanks_and_Chaplin%2C_Wall_Street_Rally%2C_New_York_Times%2C_1918.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="612" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairbanks_and_Chaplin,_Wall_Street_Rally,_New_York_Times,_1918.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> stands on <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks" title="Douglas Fairbanks">Douglas Fairbanks</a>' shoulders during a rally at Wall Street in 1918.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the discipline of anthropology, the term culture represents the customs, values, morals, laws and rituals which a particular group or society shares. In the public imagination, Wall Street represents economics and finance. However, although Wall Street employees may exhibit greedy and self-interested behaviours to the public, these behaviours are justified through their own value system and social practices. Various anthropologists have conducted research on Wall Street and it is their research which can confirm the negative views of Wall Street while providing the public with information that can contribute to a better understanding of how Wall Street workers perceive themselves. Anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Karen_Ho" title="Karen Ho">Karen Ho</a>, who has conducted ethnographic research on Wall Street, states in Situating Global Capitalisms that the markets are beginning to self-regulate themselves in terms of neoliberalism.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Through the perception of the public, financial investors take on a role that has already been established for them. It is both appropriate and fitting to call Wall Street a culture because of the system of values and practices it holds onto. Moreover, anthropological insight can help improve the general public's understanding of Wall Street and in turn allow the public to appreciate the culture of Wall Street which is both logical and sensible to the workers themselves.
</p><p>Professor Katarina Sjöberg argues in <i>The Wall Street Culture</i> that within the media negative images of Wall Street are painted in terms of the district's market falls, money losses and deceitful gains.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> However, this is not what Wall Street investors are bothered by. Instead, it is the public's words and opinions which they feel mold their image.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Sjöberg notes that in the American culture, money making is of utmost importance and knowing how to make money is considered to be respectable.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, Wall Street investors prioritize their work as well as strive to climb the corporate ladder.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> They also feel obligated to maintain the image the public creates, because it strengthens their position as a Wall Street employee. They value seeing themselves as experts in their field, especially since they live in a society that values wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This justifies their acts of greed, and allows them to take part in activities often deemed as criminal because they feel as though it is expected. Moreover, they do not regret their actions because to them, it is part of being an American.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Aside from living up to the public's image, Wall Street workers justify their high salaries with an argument pointed out by Karen Ho in Disciplining Investment Bankers, Disciplining the Economy: Wall Street's Institutional Culture of Crisis, the Downsizing of Corporate America. She argues that due to the fact that the financial market is volatile in conjunction with the existence of job insecurity, Wall Street workers are compensated through their salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Situating Global Capitalisms: A View from Wall Street Investment Bankers by Karen Ho, she interviews a banker who believes that working for Wall Street puts them at the top of the hierarchal ladder in society. The banker feels that everything goes through Wall Street, in terms of loans, investments, change or growth.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> From his point of view, Wall Street values are embedded in power.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Similarly to Sjöberg's article, The Wall Street Culture, she states that as she was entering the New York Stock Exchange (NYSE) to conduct an interview, she was questioned by many employees regarding her purpose, how she gained access into their workplace without an appointment, and how she passed security.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In their view, there is a "dividing line between 'us on the inside' and 'those on the outside'".<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> These factors strengthen the power relations as well as establish a hierarchy between them as Wall Street employees, and the public.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, in Disciplining investment Bankers, Disciplining the Economy: Wall Street's Institutional Culture of Crisis and the Downsizing of "Corporate America", Karen Ho states that as we continue to learn more about Wall Street, we learn about each independent banker. As the banker brings their life experiences into the business, we can see the reasons for their actions. Each investor has a unique identity which contributes to the culture of Wall Street. Ideally, they live in the moment.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>  However, job insecurity and the volatile nature of the market creates a constant state of fear within the investor.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, they must organize themselves and follow a pattern to ensure security, profit and prosperity for the long run.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Karen Ho wishes for us to see Wall Street through the lens of the everyday investor and banker, as well as understand the experiences and everyday situations that they must endure. Ho also believes that the individuals within the public can counteract the stereotypes and negativity that both the media and society associates with Wall Street by learning more about the personal experiences of the investors and their everyday lives.<sup id="cite_ref-Sjöberg,_K._2004_94-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sjöberg,_K._2004-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Similarly to regular wage earners, Wall Street employees are just trying to earn a day's pay. Their work is sometimes undervalued, because the public does not see them in this manner. Thus, Wall Street cannot be understood in black and white terms. One needs to understand that they have a value system which is not only logical to them, but also reflective of North America's values of individual power, prestige, and social practices based on individualism. For example, throughout the 1940s and into the 1950s Manhattan was a "white-only" community.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  Within that time period, there was a lot of racial segregation.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The values of America and the social practices were not like they are today, so African Americans were not within the Wall Street community.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s classic short story "<a href="/wiki/Bartleby,_the_Scrivener" title="Bartleby, the Scrivener">Bartleby, the Scrivener</a>" (first published in 1853 and republished in revised edition in 1856) is subtitled "A Story of Wall Street" and portrays the alienating forces at work within the confines of Wall Street.</li>
<li>Many events of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Wolfe" title="Tom Wolfe">Tom Wolfe</a>'s 1987 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bonfire_of_the_Vanities" title="The Bonfire of the Vanities">The Bonfire of the Vanities</a></i> center on Wall Street and its culture.</li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_(1987_film)" title="Wall Street (1987 film)">Wall Street</a></i> (1987) and its sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street:_Money_Never_Sleeps" title="Wall Street: Money Never Sleeps">Wall Street: Money Never Sleeps</a></i> (2010) exemplify many popular conceptions of Wall Street as a center of shady corporate dealings and <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a></i> universe, the <a href="/wiki/Ferengi" title="Ferengi">Ferengi</a> are said to make regular pilgrimages to Wall Street, which they worship as a holy site of commerce and business.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>On January 26, 2000, the band <a href="/wiki/Rage_Against_The_Machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Rage Against The Machine">Rage Against The Machine</a> filmed the music video for "Sleep Now in the Fire" on Wall Street, which was directed by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The New York Stock Exchange closed early that day, at 2:52&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Rises" title="The Dark Knight Rises">The Dark Knight Rises</a></i> (2012), <a href="/wiki/Bane_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bane (comics)">Bane</a> attacks the <a href="/wiki/Gotham_City" title="Gotham City">Gotham City</a> Stock Exchange. Scenes were filmed in and around the New York Stock Exchange, with the J.P. Morgan Building at Wall Street and Broad Street standing in for the Exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wolf_of_Wall_Street_(2013_film)" title="The Wolf of Wall Street (2013 film)">The Wolf of Wall Street</a></i> (2013) is a <a href="/wiki/Black_comedy" title="Black comedy">black comedy</a> about <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Belfort" title="Jordan Belfort">Jordan Belfort</a>, a New York stockbroker who ran <a href="/wiki/Stratton_Oakmont" title="Stratton Oakmont">Stratton Oakmont</a>, a firm that engaged in securities fraud and corruption on Wall Street from 1987 to 1998.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Personalities_associated_with_the_street">Personalities associated with the street</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Personalities associated with the street">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many people associated with Wall Street have become famous; although in most cases their reputations are limited to members of the <a href="/wiki/Brokerage_firm" class="mw-redirect" title="Brokerage firm">stock brokerage</a> and banking communities, others have gained national and international fame. For some, their fame is due to skillful investment strategies, financing, reporting, legal or regulatory activities, while others are remembered for their notable failures or scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-test_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG/250px-Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG/375px-Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG/500px-Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2780" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pier_11_at_Wall_Street_ferry.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pier 11</div></div></div>
<p>With Wall Street being historically a commuter destination, a plethora of transportation infrastructure has been developed to serve it.  <a href="/wiki/Pier_11/Wall_Street" title="Pier 11/Wall Street">Pier 11</a> at Wall Street's eastern end is a busy terminal for <a href="/wiki/New_York_Waterway" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Waterway">New York Waterway</a>, <a href="/wiki/NYC_Ferry" title="NYC Ferry">NYC Ferry</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_Water_Taxi" title="New York Water Taxi">New York Water Taxi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/SeaStreak" title="SeaStreak">SeaStreak</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Manhattan_Heliport" title="Downtown Manhattan Heliport">Downtown Manhattan Heliport</a> serves Wall Street as well.
</p><p>There are three <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">New York City Subway</a> stations under Wall Street:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_station_(IRT_Broadway%E2%80%93Seventh_Avenue_Line)" title="Wall Street station (IRT Broadway–Seventh Avenue Line)">Wall Street</a> at <a href="/wiki/William_Street_(Manhattan)" title="William Street (Manhattan)">William Street</a> (<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="2 (New York City Subway service)">2</a></span>&#32;and&#32;&#8203;<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/3_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="3 (New York City Subway service)">3</a></span>&#32;trains)<sup id="cite_ref-submap_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-submap-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_station_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="Wall Street station (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">Wall Street</a> at <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">Broadway</a> (<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/4_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="4 (New York City Subway service)">4</a></span>&#32;and&#32;&#8203;<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/5_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="5 (New York City Subway service)">5</a></span>&#32;trains)<sup id="cite_ref-submap_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-submap-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_station_(BMT_Nassau_Street_Line)" title="Broad Street station (BMT Nassau Street Line)">Broad Street</a> at <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Broad Street (Manhattan)">Broad Street</a>, with an entrance at Wall Street (<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/J_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" class="mw-redirect" title="J (New York City Subway service)">J</a></span>&#32;and&#32;&#8203;<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Z_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" class="mw-redirect" title="Z (New York City Subway service)">Z</a></span>&#32;trains)<sup id="cite_ref-submap_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-submap-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wall_Street&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
